Multiple Prostrations for the Same Verse in One Sitting
Explains when multiple prostrations merge into one due to same sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tadākhul to determine the number of prostrations required when a prostration verse is recited multiple times in one sitting.

Core Idea
One prostration suffices for multiple recitations of the same prostration verse in a single sitting.

When you recite a prostration verse more than once in the same sitting, does each recitation demand a separate prostration? The Hanafi school answers with a clear principle: merging (tadākhul) applies, provided the sitting (majlis) remains unchanged. This ruling is based on the practice of early Muslims (tābiʿūn) and avoids unnecessary hardship. In this lesson, we examine the evidence and the crucial condition that governs this merging.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is sajdat al-tilāwah (prostration of recitation). By analogy (qiyas), each recitation of a prostration verse triggers a separate wajib prostration. However, the Hanafi school applies tadākhul (merging) to combine them due to hardship.
Default qiyas is overridden by istihsan based on the practice of the salaf.
2
Understand the condition of tadākhul
Tadākhul applies only if the sitting (majlis) remains the same. If the sitting changes (e.g., the person moves to another room or his location changes significantly), each recitation requires a separate prostration.
The unity of majlis is the key condition.
3
Analyze the evidence from the tābiʿūn
Four athar are cited from the Musannaf of Ibn Abī Shayba: (1) Abū ʿAbd al-Raḥmān would recite the prostration verse, prostrate once, then repeat the recitation without prostrating again. (2) Mujāhid said, 'If you recite a prostration verse, it suffices you to prostrate once.' (3 and 4) Ibrāhīm al-Nakhaʿī stated that the first prostration suffices for subsequent recitations in the same sitting.
These are qawl sahabi (sayings of the tābiʿūn), which are a valid source in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply the condition to a changed sitting
If the same verse is recited in a new sitting (e.g., after moving to another room or after a significant gap), merging does not apply. Each recitation in a new sitting requires its own prostration.
The passage explicitly states: 'if the sitting changes, then multiple prostrations become obligatory.'
5
Note the practical consequence and ikhtilaf
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that one prostration is wajib for multiple recitations in one sitting. Other schools (such as the Shafiʿi) may require separate prostrations, but the Hanafi school follows the evidence of the salaf and the principle of tadākhul to avoid hardship.
The ruling is considered mukhtalaf fih, but the Hanafi position is relied upon.

وجه : (۱) قیاس کے اعتبار سے ہر آیت پڑھنے کے لئے الگ الگ سجدہ واجب ہونا چاہئے۔ لیکن حرج کے لئے تداخل کر دیا جائے گا۔ لیکن اس میں شرط یہ ہے کہ مجلس ایک ہو۔ دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ ادنی اعلی میں داخل ہوگا لیکن اعلی ادنی میں داخل نہیں ہوگا۔ اب اوپر کی صورت میں ایک ہی آیت کئی مرتبہ پڑھی ہے تو اگر مجلس ایک ہے تو تداخل ہو کر ایک ہی سجدہ لازم ہوگا۔ اور مجلس بدل گئی تو کئی سجدے لازم ہونگے ۔ (۲) اس قول تابعی میں ہے۔ عن ابی عبد الرحمن انه كان يقرأ السجدة فيسجد ثم يعيدها في مجلسه ذلك مرارا لا يسجد - (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۲۰۴، باب الرجل يقرا السجدة ثم يعيد قرأتها كيف يصنع ، ج اول ص ۱۳۶۶ نمبر ۴۲۰ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ایک مجلس میں کئی مرتبہ آیت سجدہ پڑھا تو تداخل ہوگا اور ایک ہی سجدہ لا زم ہوگا۔ (۳) عن مجاهد قال اذا قرأت السجدة اجزاك ان تسجد بها مرة ، (۴) عن ابراهيم في الرجل يقرأ السجدة ثم يعيد قرأتها قالا تجزيها السجدة الاولى. (مصنف بن ابى شيبة ۲۰۴ ، باب الرجل يقرا السجدة ثم يعيد قر آ تھا کیف یصنع ج اول ص ۱۳۶۵، نمبر ۴۱۹۹/۴۲۰) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کئی مرتبہ آیت سجدہ پڑھنے سے اگر مجلس ایک ہو تو ایک ہی سجدہ کافی ہے۔
Wajh (1): According to qiyas, for each verse recited, a separate prostration should be obligatory. But due to hardship, merging (tadākhul) is applied. However, the condition is that the sitting (majlis) is one. Secondly, the lesser is included in the greater, but the greater is not included in the lesser. Now, in the above scenario, if the same verse is recited multiple times, then if the sitting is one, merging takes place and only one prostration is obligatory. If the sitting changes, then multiple prostrations become obligatory. (2) This is in the saying of a Tābiʿī: from Abī ʿAbd al-Raḥmān that he would recite a prostration verse and prostrate, then recite it again in that same sitting multiple times and not prostrate. (Musannaf Ibn Abī Shayba, 204, Bāb al-Rajul Yaqraʾ al-Sajda Thumma Yuʿīdu Qirāʾatahā Kayfa Yaṣnaʿ, vol. 1, p. 1366, no. 420). From this athar it is understood that if a prostration verse is recited multiple times in one sitting, merging occurs and one prostration is obligatory. (3) From Mujāhid, he said: 'If you recite a prostration verse, it suffices you to prostrate once.' (4) From Ibrāhīm, concerning a man who recites a prostration verse and then repeats its recitation, they said: 'The first prostration suffices for it.' (Musannaf Ibn Abī Shayba, 204, Bāb, vol. 1, p. 1365, nos. 4199/420). From this athar it is understood that if a prostration verse is recited multiple times, if the sitting is one, one prostration is sufficient.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from Thamaratun Najah addresses the ruling when a prostration verse is recited multiple times in one sitting. The author explains that by qiyas (analogy) each recitation would require a separate prostration, but the principle of tadākhul (merging) is applied to avoid hardship, provided the sitting (majlis) remains the same. The condition of unity of sitting is crucial: if the sitting changes, separate prostrations become obligatory. The author supports this with four athar (traditions) from the tābiʿīn, all from the Musannaf of Ibn Abī Shayba, showing that the early Muslims would not repeat the prostration when reciting the verse multiple times in one sitting. The Hanafi ruling is clear: one prostration suffices for multiple recitations in the same sitting.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The sitting session; the continuous period a person remains in one place while reciting or listening.
تداخل (tadākhul): Merging of multiple obligatory acts into one due to a common cause or hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person recites the same prostration verse twice in one sitting, how many prostrations are obligatory?
A) Two prostrations
B) One prostration
C) No prostration is required
D) It depends on the number of recitations

Key Takeaway
For multiple recitations of the same prostration verse in one sitting, one prostration suffices; the key is the unity of the sitting (majlis).

Actions That Do Not Change the Legal Sitting for Prostration
Lists twelve actions that do not alter the legal sitting for prostration obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions do not change the majlis for prostration of recitation according to the Hanafi school and apply the principle to similar cases.

Core Idea
Minor actions like moving steps, eating, praying do not change the sitting; only a significant change triggers a new prostration obligation.

This lesson lists specific actions that the Hanafi school considers too minor to change the legal session (majlis) for the obligation of prostration of recitation. Understanding these exceptions prevents unnecessary repetition of prostrations and clarifies the concept of continuity in worship. The underlying principle is that customary small movements do not break the unity of the session.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of majlis
The majlis is the place or session in which a person is present for recitation. A change in majlis can multiply the obligation to prostrate. This lesson defines what constitutes a change.
2
Memorize the list of exceptions
Study the twelve actions listed: moving to corners of a building, sailing, praying one or two rak'ahs, drinking, eating one or two morsels, walking one or two steps, leaning, sitting, standing, riding, dismounting, and the mount moving while praying.
These are all considered minor movements.
3
Identify the common principle
All listed actions are customary ('urfi) minor movements that do not signify a departure from the session. The criterion is that they are insignificant in common understanding.
4
Apply to new scenarios
Use the principle to evaluate other borderline actions. For example, walking three steps would likely still be minor; walking to another room may be a change. The majority of Hanafi scholars consider less than three steps as not changing the majlis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتبدّل بزوايا بالبيت والمسجد ولو كبيرا ولا بسير سفينة ولا بركعة وبركعتين وشربة واكل لقمتين ومشى خطوتين ولا باتكاء وقعود وقيام ووركوب ونزول في محل تلاوته ولا بسير دابته مصليا
And the sitting does not change by (moving to) corners of the house and mosque, even if large; nor by the sailing of a ship; nor by praying one or two rakʿahs; nor by drinking; nor by eating one or two morsels; nor by walking one or two steps; nor by leaning, nor by sitting, nor by standing; nor by riding and descending at the place of his recitation; nor by the walking of his mount while he is praying.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage from Thamaratun Najah lists twelve actions that, according to the Hanafi school, do not change the legal sitting (majlis) with respect to the obligation of prostration of recitation. The ruling is that as long as these minor movements occur, the sitting remains the same, and if a prostration verse was recited earlier, the obligation is not multiplied. The list includes moving within a building, traveling in a ship, performing a few rakʿahs, eating, drinking, walking a few steps, changing posture, riding, and even the mount's movement during prayer. The underlying principle is that these actions do not constitute a significant change of place in customary understanding ('urf). This ruling facilitates worship by preventing multiple prostrations due to trivial movements.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The place or session in which a person is present for recitation; its change affects the obligation to repeat prostration.
زوايا (zawāyā): Corners of a house or mosque; moving to different corners does not change the sitting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions does NOT change the sitting for prostration according to this lesson?
A) Moving to a different room in the mosque
B) Walking one step
C) Sailing on a ship
D) Eating three morsels

Key Takeaway
Minor customary movements do not change the legal sitting for prostration; only significant changes multiply the obligation.

Repetition of Prostration Obligation for the Listener
Ruling on repetition of listener's prostration obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for repetition of prostration obligation upon the listener and identify whose change of sitting triggers the repetition.

Core Idea
Only the listener's own change of sitting multiplies his obligation — not the reciter's.

This lesson explains ruling (769) from Thamaratun Najah, which clarifies when a listener must perform multiple prostrations after hearing repeated recitations of a prostration verse. The key distinction is between a change in the listener's own sitting and a change in the reciter's.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and persons involved
The act is sujud al-tilawah (prostration of recitation). The persons are al-tali (reciter) and al-sami' (listener). Both are obliged to perform prostration upon hearing/reciting a prostration verse.
2
Determine the condition for repetition
Repetition of wajib occurs upon a change of sitting (tabdil al-majlis). The text says the obligation repeats (yatakarrar al-wujub) when the listener changes his sitting while the reciter's sitting remains the same.
Change of sitting means physically moving from one place to another, not merely a posture change.
3
Apply the condition to each person
If the listener's own sitting changes, his obligation repeats. If only the reciter's sitting changes, the listener's obligation does not repeat.
4
Recognize the exception
The opposite (reciter's change affecting listener) is not the case — explicitly stated as 'la bi'aksihi'.
5
Note the authority: 'ala al-ashah'
The phrase 'ala al-ashah' (according to the most correct view) indicates that there is an intra-Hanafi difference of opinion, but the relied-upon view is as stated.
The mufta bihi is that only the listener's own change triggers repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۶۹) ويتكرّر الوجوب على السامع بتبديل مجلسه وقد اتحد مجلس التالى لا بعكسه على الاصح (۷۷۰) وكره ان يقرأ سورة ويدع آية السجدة لا عكسه وندب ضم آية او اكثر اليها
(769) And the obligation is repeated upon the listener by his change of sitting while the sitting of the reciter is one, not vice versa, according to the most correct view. (770) And it is disliked (makruh) that he recites a surah and leaves out a prostration verse, but not the opposite; and it is recommended (mandub) to join a verse or more to it.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings from Thamaratun Najah. The first (769) deals with the listener's obligation for prostration when his own sitting changes while the reciter's sitting remains the same. In such a case, the listener must perform multiple prostrations, one for each time his sitting changes. However, if the reciter's sitting changes but the listener's remains the same, the listener's obligation does not multiply—only the reciter's does. The principle is that repetition of obligation is tied to the change of one's own sitting, not another's. The second ruling (770) states that it is makruh (reprehensible) for a person reciting the Quran to deliberately skip a prostration verse out of fear or disinterest in prostrating, but the opposite (reciting only the prostration verse and skipping the rest of the surah) is not disliked. However, it is recommended to join one or two verses with the prostration verse so that it is not recited in isolation. This is because isolating the prostration verse may give the impression of avoiding it, whereas the proper etiquette is to recite it as part of the Quran.

Key Terms
سامع (sāmiʿ): The listener who hears the prostration verse.
تالى (tālī): The reciter, the one who recites the prostration verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to ruling (769), if the reciter changes his sitting but the listener remains in the same place, how many prostrations does the listener need to perform?
A) Multiple prostrations
B) One prostration
C) No prostration
D) It depends on the number of recitations

Key Takeaway
The repetition of prostration obligation is personal: only a change in the listener's own sitting triggers multiple prostrations.

Skipping a Prostration Verse While Reciting
Ruling on skipping prostration verse and joining verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on deliberately skipping a prostration verse when reciting a surah, and explain why it is recommended to join other verses.

Core Idea
Skipping a prostration verse while reciting a surah is disliked; better to include it with surrounding verses.

This lesson addresses ruling (770), which covers two points: first, it is makruh to recite a surah and purposely omit a prostration verse; second, it is recommended to add one or more verses alongside the prostration verse to avoid reciting it in isolation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
The act is reciting a surah while deliberately skipping a prostration verse. The ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked).
Makruh tanzihi means it is not sinful but avoided by pious people.
2
Understand the exception
The opposite—reciting only the prostration verse and skipping the rest of the surah—is not makruh. The text says 'la 'aksahu' (not its opposite).
3
Recognize the recommended practice
It is recommended (mandub) to join one or two additional verses to the prostration verse when reciting it, so that it is not recited alone.
The wisdom is to avoid isolating the prostration verse, which may give the impression of avoiding the sajdah.
4
Discern the wisdom behind the ruling
The karaha stems from improper adab (etiquette) with the Quran; skipping a prostration verse deliberately shows lack of reverence. Joining other verses preserves the context and shows respect.

Source Text

Repetition of Sujud al-Tilawa When Listener Changes Session
Ruling on repetition of sujud when listener changes session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on repetition of sujud al-tilawa for a listener when sessions change and explain the underlying principle.

Core Idea
The obligation of sujud al-tilawa repeats for the one whose session changes, not for the one who stays stationary.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in the chapter of sujud al-tilawa: when a listener hears an ayah sajda multiple times but changes his session between recitations. The ruling hinges on the concept of session change (tabdīl al-majlis), which is crucial for determining whether the obligation is repeated. We will explore the Hanafi position and the principle that governs this repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key elements
Distinguish between the reciter and the listener. Note their respective sessions: the reciter's session may be fixed or changing, and the listener's session may be fixed or changing.
Session change (tabdīl majlis) means physically moving to a different location or a significant change in position recognized by custom.
2
State the ruling for each combination
Apply the rule: repetition of sujud al-tilawa follows the person whose session changes. List the four possible combinations: (1) reciter fixed, listener changes → listener repeats; (2) reciter changes, listener fixed → listener does not repeat (reciter repeats); (3) both fixed → no repetition; (4) both change → each repeats according to their own session changes.
The text explicitly covers cases 1 and 2.
3
Understand the basis of the ruling
The ruling is based on the principle that each new session creates a fresh obligation for the person who is present in that session. The listener’s obligation is tied to his own session, not the reciter’s.
This principle is consistent with Hanafi usul where obligation is linked to the individual's circumstances.
4
Recognize the differing view
The text states ‘alā al-aṣaḥḥ (on the correct view), indicating that there is an alternative view. The relied-upon Hanafi position is as stated.
Other madhabs may not differentiate based on session change, considering the hearing of the verse itself sufficient for obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود التلاوة وركوب ونزول في محل تلاوته ولا بسير دابته مصليا . (۷۶۹) ويتكرّر الوجوب على السامع بتبديل مجلسه وقد اتحد مجلس التالى لا بعكسه على الاصح
ہی جگہ بیٹھا ہوا ہے اس لئے اس کی مجلس نہیں بدلے گی۔ وجه : یہ سب صورتیں ایسی ہیں کہ آدمی یا تو جگہ نہیں بدلتا، یا تھوڑ اسا بدلتا ہے جس کو عرف میں بدلنا نہیں کہتے ۔ اس لئے ان صورتوں میں مجلس نہیں بدلے گی۔ لغت : زوایا: زاویہ کی جمع ہے، گھر کا کونہ۔سیر: چلنا۔ دابتہ : چوپایہ سواری۔ خطوتین : خطوة کی تثنیہ ہے، ایک دو قدم ۔ اتکاء: ٹھیک ترجمه : (۷۶۹) سننے والے پر سجدے کا وجوب مکرر ہوگا اس کی مجلس بدلنے سے حال یہ کہ پڑھنے والے کی مجلس ایک ہے، نہ کہ اس کا الٹا پیچ رویت پر ۔ تشریح : آیت پڑھنے والے کی مجلس ایک ہے اور سننے والے کی مجلس بدلتی جارہی ہے تو سامع پر مکر رسجدہ واجب ہوگا، کیونکہ خود سامع کی مجلس بدلتی جارہی ہے ۔ اور اس کا الٹا یہ ہے کہ پڑھنے والے کی مجلس بدلتی جارہی ہے وہ ادھر سے ادھر گھوم رہا ہے اور سننے والے کی مجلس ایک ہے تو سننے والے پر ایک ہی سجدہ لازم ہوگا ، کیونکہ اس کی مجلس ایک ہی ہے۔ اور پڑھنے والے پر مکر رسجدے ہوں گے، کیونکہ اس کی مجلس بدل رہی ہے۔ اصول : جس کی مجلس بدلے گی اسی پر مکر رسجدہ لازم ہوگا۔
Chapter of Prostration of Recitation. And riding and descending in his place of recitation, and not by the walking of his mount while praying. (769) And the obligation is repeated for the listener by his changing of his session while the session of the reciter is one, not the opposite on the correct view. ... [Urdu: He is sitting in one place, so his session will not change. Explanation: All these are situations where a person either does not change place, or changes slightly such that in custom it is not considered changing. Therefore the session does not change in these cases. Lexicon: ... Translation (769): The obligation of prostration will be repeated for the listener due to his changing of his session, even if the reciter's session is one, and not the opposite on the correct view. Explanation: If the reciter's session is one and the listener's session is changing, then repeated prostration is obligatory for the listener because his own session is changing. The opposite is if the reciter's session is changing and the listener's session is one, then only one prostration is incumbent on the listener because his session is one, but repeated prostrations are incumbent on the reciter because his session is changing. Principle.: The one whose session changes will have repeated prostrations.]
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Chapter on the Prostration of Recitation. The passage begins by listing some cases where a person does not change his session while reciting or listening, such as riding or descending in the same place. Then it states the main ruling: the obligation of prostration of recitation is repeated for the listener if he changes his session, even if the reciter remains in one session. The opposite case is not true: if the reciter changes his session but the listener remains in one session, then the listener only owes one prostration while the reciter owes repeated prostrations. The principle is that repetition of obligation is tied to the person whose session changes. This ruling is from the Hanafi school and is the relied-upon position ('alā al-aṣaḥḥ).

Key Terms
سجود التلاوة (sujūd al-tilāwa): The prostration performed when reciting or hearing a verse of prostration (āyat sajda) in the Quran.
مجلس (majlis): The sitting place or session; changing of session (tabdīl majlis) means physically moving to a different location or significantly changing one's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a reciter recites an āyat sajda while walking from one room to another (changing session), and a listener stays in one place, how many prostrations does the listener owe?
A) One prostration
B) Repeated prostrations for each new session of the reciter
C) None, because the reciter's session changed
D) Two prostrations

Key Takeaway
The obligation of sujud al-tilawa repeats for the person whose session changes, not for the person who remains stationary.

Ruling on Skipping a Verse of Prostration
Ruling on omitting a prostration verse while reciting a surah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on skipping a verse of prostration when reciting a surah, distinguish it from the case of reciting only the verse, and identify the evidence from the Salaf.

Core Idea
Skipping a verse of prostration is makruh; reciting only the verse is not—honor the verse, don't avoid it.

Reciting the Quran with proper etiquette includes how we handle verses of prostration. This lesson examines a specific rule: what if someone recites a surah but deliberately omits the verse of prostration? And what about the opposite—reciting only that verse? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on a report from al-Sha'bi, which sheds light on the early practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act in question is reciting a surah while deliberately skipping the verse of prostration (e.g., out of fear or laziness to prostrate).
Also consider the opposite: reciting only the verse of prostration without the rest of the surah.
2
State the Ruling
Skipping the verse of prostration is makruh tanzihi (disliked) because it implies avoidance. The opposite—reciting only the verse—is not disliked; it honors the verse.
The Hanafi position is based on the athar from al-Sha'bi.
3
Understand the Recommendation
It is mustahabb (recommended) to attach one or two additional verses before or after the prostration verse so it is not recited alone.
This avoids any resemblance to avoiding the verse.
4
Examine the Evidence
The proof is a mursal hadith from al-Sha'bi in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba: 'They used to dislike abbreviating the prostration and they used to dislike that when they came upon a prostration they would pass it by until they prostrated.'
The word 'dislike' indicates karaha tanzihiyya in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۷۷۰) وكره ان يقرأ سورة ويدع آية السجدة لا عكسه وندب ضم آية او اكثر اليها
ترجمه : ( ۷۷۰) اور سورت پڑھ کر آیت سجدہ چھوڑ دینا مکروہ ہے۔ نہ کہ اس کا الٹا۔ مگر ہاں اس کے ساتھ ایک یا دو آیتیں ملا لینا مستحب ہے۔ تشریح : سورت پڑھے اور سجدہ کرنے کے ڈر سے آیت سجدہ چھوڑ دے یہ مکروہ ہے، کیونکہ آیت سجدہ سے احتراز ہے۔اور اس کا الٹا، یعنی صرف آیت سجدہ پڑھے اور باقی آیتوں کو چھوڑ دے اس میں کوئی حرج کی بات نہیں ہے، کیونکہ آیت سجدہ ، اور سجدہ کی عزت ہے۔ البتہ بہتر یہ ہے کہ آیت سجدہ کے ساتھ ایک دو آیتیں ملالے، تا کہ وہ تنہا نہ رہے۔ وجه : (۱) اسکی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس سے شبہ ہوتا ہے کہ آیت سجدہ سے منہ موڑ رہے ہیں اور اسکو پڑھنا نہیں چاہتے ہیں،اسلئے اسکو چھوڑ نا مکروہ ہے۔ (۲) اس اثر میں اسکا ثبوت ہے۔ عن الشعبي قال : كانوا يكرهون اختصار السجود و كانوا يكرهون اذا اتوا على السجدة أن يجاوزوها حتى يسجدوا (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ، باب فی اختصار السجود، ج اول، ص ۱۳۶۶ نمبر ۴۲۰۳ ) اس اثر میں ہے کہ آیت سجدہ کو چھوڑ دینا اچھا نہیں سمجھتے تھے۔
(770) And it is disliked that he recites a surah and leaves out a verse of prostration, not the opposite, and it is recommended to attach one or more verses to it. Translation (770): And reciting a surah and skipping a verse of prostration is disliked, not the opposite. However, it is recommended to add one or two verses with it. Explanation: Reciting a surah and skipping a verse of prostration out of fear of prostrating is disliked because it is avoidance of the verse of prostration. The opposite, i.e., reciting only the verse of prostration and leaving out the rest, there is no harm in it because the verse of prostration and the prostration are honored. However, it is better to add one or two verses with it so that it does not remain alone. Reason: (1) The reason is that it gives the impression of turning away from the verse of prostration and not wanting to recite it, so skipping it is disliked. (2) This is proven by the athar: From al-Sha'bi, he said: They used to dislike abbreviating the prostration and they used to dislike that when they came upon a prostration they would pass it by until they prostrated. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, Chapter on abbreviating the prostration, vol. 1, p. 1366, no. 4203) This athar indicates that skipping the verse of prostration was not considered good.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is a ruling on the etiquette of reciting Quran in relation to verses of prostration. The text states that it is makruh (disliked) for a person to recite a surah but deliberately omit the verse of prostration within it, for fear of having to prostrate. The opposite—reciting only the verse of prostration and omitting the rest of the surah—is not disliked; in fact, it shows honor to the verse. However, it is mustahabb (recommended) to add one or two verses before or after the verse of prostration so that it is not recited alone. The reasoning is that skipping the verse gives the impression of avoiding prostration. The proof is an athar from al-Sha'bi that the early Muslims disapproved of 'abbreviating the prostration' (ikhtiṣār al-sujūd), meaning passing over the verse without prostrating.

Key Terms
اختصار السجود (ikhtiṣār al-sujūd): Abbreviating the prostration, i.e., skipping the verse of prostration or not prostrating when coming to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on reciting only the verse of prostration and omitting the rest of the surah?
A) It is makruh
B) It is haram
C) There is no harm in it
D) It is wajib to add other verses

Key Takeaway
Skipping a verse of prostration is makruh tanzihi; reciting the verse alone is permissible but it is better to include additional verses to avoid the appearance of avoidance.
